\begin{itemize}

So sunny lines are those that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1 (i.e., not parallel to x+y=0). Actually line x+y=0 has slope -1. So sunny lines are lines that are not parallel to any of these three lines. So they cannot have slope 0 (horizontal), infinite slope (vertical), or slope -1. We need to determine all nonnegative integers k (depending on n) such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering the specified points.

Now, what about k=3 for n=6? We already argued impossible. So for n=6, the only possible k are 0 and 1. But is k=0 possible? Yes, with 6 non-sunny lines covering S_6, e.g., all anti-diagonals D3..D7 (5 lines) but we need 6 lines. We can use D3..D7 (5 lines) and then add one more non-sunny line (like H1 or V1) that is redundant. That would give k=0, but we need exactly n lines. So we can take the 5 anti-diagonals covering all points, and then add a sixth line that is also non-sunny (like another anti-diagonal D8, but D8 has no points, but we can still include it as a line; it's allowed because lines are just lines, they don't have to intersect S. However the condition "exactly k of the n lines are sunny" means that among the n lines, exactly k are sunny. If we include a line that is not sunny, then it's fine. So we can take the 5 anti-diagonals (non-sunny) covering S_6, and then add any non-sunny line (like x=0, or y=0, or D8) that is not sunny. That gives a configuration with 6 lines, all non-sunny, k=0. That's valid. So k=0 is possible.

But is there a configuration with k=1 for n=6? Yes, we can use 5 non-sunny lines covering S_6 and add one sunny line (redundant). That's k=1. So k=1 is possible. So for n=6, possible k are 0,1. That's a set of size 2. For n=3,4,5, possible k are 0,1,3. So there is a transition. For n=7, we need to check.

The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:" So we need to give an answer based on n. It seems the answer might be: for n=3, k∈{0,1,3}; for n=4,5, k∈{0,1,3}; for n≥6, k∈{0,1} (or maybe also k=2 for some n?). But we need to prove that for n≥6, k=2 and k=3 are impossible. Also check if k=n is impossible for n≥4, which we already argued. So the possible k are 0,1 for n≥6, but also 3 for n=4,5. That's interesting. But we also need to consider n=3,4,5 where 3 is possible. So the answer may be:

- If n = 3, then k can be 0, 1, 3.
- If n = 4 or 5, then k can be 0, 1, 3.
- If n ≥ 6, then k can be 0, 1.

But is k=2 possible for any n? We suspect not, but we need to prove it in general. Let's analyze the geometry more abstractly.

We have S_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a≥1, b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1}. The lines are in the plane. A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the lines: x-axis (slope 0), y-axis (infinite slope), x+y=0 (slope -1). So sunny lines have slopes m ∈ ℝ ∪ {∞} such that m ∉ {0, ∞, -1} (and m ≠ ∞ for vertical lines? Actually vertical lines have slope ∞, so they are not sunny). So sunny lines have slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1.

Now, we need to find, for a given n, which k are achievable. The examples:

- k=0: take n horizontal lines y = 1,2,…,n. These are all non-sunny. Do they cover S_n? For any point (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1, b ≤ n, so it is covered by y=b. Yes. So k=0 is possible.

- k=1: take n-1 horizontal lines (y=1,…,n-1) and one sunny line, e.g., y = x (slope 1) that covers the point (1,n). But we need to ensure that all points with b=n are covered; the points with b=n are (1,n) only (since a≥1, a+b ≤ n+1 → a ≤ n+1 - n = 1). So (1,n) is covered by y=n (non-sunny). So to have exactly 1 sunny line, we could use all horizontals except one, and then replace that one with a sunny line that covers the points not covered by the other horizontals? Actually if we use horizontals y=1,…,n-1, then points with b=n (which is (1,n)) are not covered, so we need to cover it. We can add a sunny line that passes through (1,n) and is sunny. That's one sunny line. But we have n lines total; we used n-1 horizontals and 1 sunny line, total n lines. That's valid. So k=1 is possible.

- k=n: all lines sunny. For n=3, we gave an example. For n≥4, we need to see if it's possible. As argued, with n sunny lines, each line can contain at most 3 points from S_n? Actually the maximum number of points from S_n on a sunny line depends on n. For n≥4, we saw that there exist sunny lines with 3 points. For n≥6, there are sunny lines with 3 points. But can we have n sunny lines covering all points? The total points is n(n+1)/2. If we have n lines, each can cover at most some number. The maximum coverage per line is something like the size of the largest collinear subset of S_n that is not parallel to forbidden directions. But we need to compute an upper bound.

Let's try to derive a general bound. For a given n, consider the set of points (a,b) with a+b = c (c ≤ n+1). The size of that anti-diagonal is c-1. The anti-diagonal with c = n+1 has size n. That's a set of points all on a line of slope -1, which is not sunny. So any sunny line cannot contain all those points. But it could contain some of them. However, due to the structure, it's plausible that a sunny line can contain at most n points? That's not helpful. But we can try to bound the number of points on a sunny line by something like n (since it's a line, it can intersect the triangular grid in a finite set). The maximum possible number of points from S_n on a line with slope not 0,∞,-1 is something like: consider a line with slope 1, it will contain points where a=b, up to floor((n+1)/2). That's about n/2. For slope -2, it's about n/2 as well. For slope -1/2, it's about n/2. So at most about n/2. Actually we can get up to n points? For n=6, we got 3 points on slope -2. That's n/2. For n=7, we might get 3 or 4? Let's compute. For n=7, S_7 has 28 points. The anti-diagonal a+b=8 has 7 points. Sunny lines could have up to 3 points maybe. But could there be a sunny line with 4 points? For n=7, consider line y = -2x + 10? That passes through (1,8) (sum9) not in S? Actually a+b≤8, so (1,8) sum=9>8 not allowed. But we need to consider points with a+b ≤ n+1 = 8. The line y = -2x + 8 passes through (1,6) sum7, (2,4) sum6, (3,2) sum5, (4,0) not. That's 3 points. So still 3. So seems max is 3 for n≥6? For n=5, we had a line with 3 points. So the maximum number of points on a sunny line in S_n is:

- If n ≤ 3, it's at most 2.
- If n ≥ 4, it's at most 3? Actually for n=4, we found a line with 3 points (y=-2x+7). That's 3 points. So indeed for n≥4, there exist sunny lines with 3 points. For n=3, max is 2. So for n≥4, the maximum is 3. But is it possible that for n large, we can have a sunny line with 4 points? Let's try to find a line with slope, say, -1/2 that passes through many integer points with a+b ≤ n+1. For a line with slope -1/2, integer points occur when the differences are (2, -1) or (2, -1) but with slope -1/2, the line equation y = -1/2 x + c. For integer x, y is integer when c is half-integer? Actually for x even, y is integer if c is integer or half-integer? Let's solve: y = -1/2 x + c. For integer x, y is integer iff (x mod 2) yields something. But we can also have lines with rational slopes that pass through many lattice points. The number of lattice points on a line within a triangular region is limited by the size of the region. In general, for a line with slope p/q in lowest terms, the integer points are spaced by (q,p). So the maximum number of points in a line of length L is about L / max(|p|,|q|) etc. But we can compute an upper bound: Since S_n is contained in the rectangle 1≤a≤n, 1≤b≤n, but not all points. Actually a can be up to n, b up to n. But points with a+b ≤ n+1 are a subset. A line that is not parallel to axes or slope -1 can contain at most something like n points? Let's think systematically.

We want to prove that for n ≥ 4, any sunny line (slope not 0,∞,-1) contains at most 3 points from S_n. But is that true for all n? Let's test n=7 with slope 1: points with a=b, a+b ≤ 8 => a ≤ 4. So (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) — that's 4 points! Wait a+b ≤ 8, so a=b ≤ 4. That's 4 points. Are these points in S_7? Yes, n=7, S_7 includes a,b≥1, a+b ≤ 8. So (4,4) sum=8, allowed. So the line y = x contains 4 points. That's a sunny line (slope 1). So that's 4 points. That's more than 3. So my earlier claim that max is 3 for n≥4 is false. Let's examine: For n=5, S_5 includes a+b ≤ 6. The line y=x has points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) — that's 3 points. For n=6, S_6 includes a+b ≤ 7. The line y=x has points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) — (4,4) sum=8 >7, so only 3. For n=7, S_7 includes a+b ≤ 8. So (4,4) is allowed, giving 4 points. So indeed, for n≥7, the line y=x can have up to floor((n+1)/2) points. That's about n/2. So a sunny line can contain many points. So my earlier argument about max coverage per sunny line is not limited to 3. That changes things.

Thus for n≥7, it's possible that all n lines are sunny and cover S_n. But is it possible to have n sunny lines covering S_n? For n=7, S_7 has 28 points. If we use 7 sunny lines, each could cover many points. The question is: can we cover all 28 points with 7 sunny lines? That seems plausible if we choose lines that are like the diagonals of a grid. But we need to check if such a covering exists. The problem is to determine all k for a given n. So we need to analyze the possible k.

Let's think about the problem more deeply. We have n lines, they can be chosen arbitrarily. The condition is that for all (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1, there exists at least one line among the n that passes through that point. So we need to cover a triangular set of lattice points.

The lines are in the plane. The non-sunny lines are those that are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. So they are "special". The sunny lines are all others.

We are to determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering S_n.

Observation: If we can cover S_n with some lines, we can add extra lines (sunny or not) to increase the count of lines, but we need exactly n lines. So we need to find a set of n lines (distinct) that covers S_n. The sunny count is the number among them that are sunny.

We can try to construct configurations for various k. The examples we have:

- k=0: n horizontals.
- k=1: n-1 horizontals + 1 sunny line.
- k=n: all lines sunny. Is this always possible? For n=3, yes. For n=4, we need 4 sunny lines covering S_4 (size 10). Can we find 4 sunny lines covering those 10 points? The line y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) is not in S_4 (since sum 6 >5). So only 2 points. The line y=2x? That covers (1,2) only. So not many. With 4 sunny lines, the maximum number of distinct points we can cover is maybe 8 (since we saw some sunny lines have at most 2 points). But we need 10. So impossible. So for n=4, k=n is impossible. For n=5, we need 5 sunny lines covering 15 points. The line y=x covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) (3 points). The line y=-2x+7 covers (1,5),(2,3),(3,1) (3 points). So we have two lines with 3 points each. But we need to cover 15 points. With 5 lines, max coverage if each has 3 points (and they are disjoint) is 15. That's exactly the size. So if we can find 5 sunny lines that together cover all points of S_5, with each line covering 3 points and no overlaps, then it's possible. But is there a set of 5 such lines? We would need 3 more lines to cover them, each with 3 points. But can we find three more sunny lines covering those 9 points? Possibly some lines with slope -1/2, -2/3 etc. But we need to check if it's possible. The problem asks to "determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both conditions." So for each n, we need to give a set of k (depending on n). The answer might be:

- If n = 3, then k can be 0, 1, 3.
- If n = 4 or 5, then k can be 0, 1, 3.
- If n ≥ 6, then k can be 0, 1, 2, 3? But we need to examine n=6,7,8,... to see if k=2 and k=3 are possible. For n=6, we saw that k=2 and k=3 are impossible. For n=7, let's test.

First, we need to see if there is a configuration with exactly 2 sunny lines and 5 non-sunny lines covering S_7. That seems plausible if we can cover many points with non-sunny lines. The maximum union with 5 non-sunny lines might be high. We need to compute the maximum possible number of points from S_n that can be covered by a set of m non-sunny lines (each of type H,V,D). Then the remaining points must be covered by the sunny lines. Since we have n lines total, with k sunny, we have n-k non-sunny lines. So the union size U of the non-sunny lines must be at least |S_n| - (max points a sunny line can cover, but we need to cover the complement). But we can also have sunny lines covering points that are also covered by non-sunny lines, so they can cover the complement.

Thus the necessary condition is that the complement R (points not covered by non-sunny lines) must be coverable by the sunny lines. The maximum number of points that k sunny lines can cover is at most k * (maximum points per sunny line). But we can also have overlapping. So we need |R| ≤ k * M, where M is the maximum number of points from S_n that can lie on a sunny line. However, M depends on n. For n≥4, M is at least something like floor((n+1)/2) (from y=x). But that's for all n≥4? For n=4, floor((4+1)/2)=2, and indeed the line y=x contains 2 points, but also we have lines with 3 points? Wait we found a line with 3 points for n=4, but that line is y=-2x+7, which is sunny and contains 3 points. So M≥3 for n≥4? For n=4, we have 3 points on y=-2x+7. So M=3. For n=5, we have 3 points on y=x (3 points) and also on y=-2x+7 (3 points). So M=3. For n=6, we have 3 points on y=x (3 points) and also on y=-2x+8 (3 points). So M=3. For n=7, we have 4 points on y=x (4 points) and also on y=-2x+? maybe 3 points. So M=4. For n=8, y=x has 4 points (since (4,4) sum8, (5,5) sum10>9 not). So M=4? Actually a+b≤9, so (4,4) sum8 allowed, (5,5) sum10>9 not, so 4 points. Also y=-2x+? could have 3 points. So M=4. For n=9, y=x has 5 points (since (5,5) sum10, (6,6) sum12>10). So M=5. So M = floor((n+1)/2) for n≥? Actually from line y=x, we get points with a=b and a+b ≤ n+1 → 2a ≤ n+1 → a ≤ floor((n+1)/2). So number of such points is floor((n+1)/2). That's one candidate. But there might be sunny lines with more points than that? For n=4, floor((4+1)/2)=2, but we found a line with 3 points (y=-2x+7). So M≥3 > floor((n+1)/2). So the maximum per sunny line is not simply floor((n+1)/2). We need to find the maximum size of a subset of S_n that is collinear and not parallel to forbidden directions. That is a combinatorial geometry problem.

Let's define the triangular grid. We have points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. We want to find the maximum number of such points that can lie on a line (with slope m ∉ {0, ∞, -1}). This is like finding the maximum number of lattice points in a triangular region on a line.

Observations: The lines that contain many points are those with rational slopes. The region is a right triangle. The maximum collinear points will be achieved by lines that are "diagonals" of a grid, but we cannot use slope -1. So we need to analyze.

We can think of the points as integer lattice points in the triangle. The line with slope 1 contains points where a=b. The number of such points is floor((n+1)/2). That's about n/2. The line with slope -2 contains points where b = -2a + c. For integer a, b is integer when c is integer. The points are (a, -2a + c). To have a,b≥1 and a+b ≤ n+1. The number of such points is about n/3 maybe. For n=6, we got 3 points with slope -2. For n=7, slope -2 gives (1,6) sum7? Actually a+b ≤8, so (1,6) sum7, (2,4) sum6, (3,2) sum5, (4,0) no. That's 3 points. So that's less than 4. So the maximum per line seems to be floor((n+1)/2) from y=x for n≥? But for n=4, floor((4+1)/2)=2, but we have a line with 3 points. So the maximum can exceed floor((n+1)/2) for small n. For n=4, we have 3 points on slope -2. For n=5, floor((5+1)/2)=3, and we have 3 points on y=x and also on y=-2x+7. So M=3. For n=6, floor((6+1)/2)=3, M=3. For n=7, floor((7+1)/2)=4, but maybe there exists a line with 4 points? y=x has 4. Also slope -2 gives 3. So M=4. So M = max(floor((n+1)/2), something else). But for n≥4, the line y=x gives floor((n+1)/2). For n=4, that's 2, but we have 3. So the maximum is larger for n=4. So M is not monotonic in n? Actually for n=4, M=3; for n=5, M=3; for n=6, M=3; for n=7, M=4; so M increases.

Thus to determine possible k, we need to consider the geometry.

The problem might have a known solution: the possible k are all integers from 0 to n, except that k = n-1 and k = n are impossible for n ≥ 6? Now, for n=3, the first n-3 = 0 rows, complement would be all points (6), we would need 3 sunny lines. That's possible as we gave example. So k=3 is achievable for n≥3.

Now, what about k=2? For n≥3, can we achieve k=2? That would require 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The complement after n-2 non-sunny lines is something. We can try to bound. That's max. Complement triple not coverable. For n=6, n-2=4 non-sunny lines, max union 18, complement 3. Not coverable. For n=7, n-2=5 non-sunny lines, max union 25, complement 3. Not coverable. So it seems k=2 is impossible for all n≥3. But we need to check n=3: n-2=1 non-sunny line, max union 3 (row b=1). Complement 3 points (b=2,3). Those 3 points are (1,2),(2,2),(1,3) maybe? Actually S_3 points: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(1,2),(2,2),(1,3). Row b=1 covers 3 points. Complement: (1,2),(2,2),(1,3). That's 3 points. Can we cover these with 2 sunny lines? As earlier, no. So k=2 impossible for n=3. So likely k=2 is impossible for all n≥3.

Now, what about k>3? For n≥4, k=4,5,...,n are impossible because with n lines total, the number of sunny lines cannot exceed something like the maximum coverage per sunny line times k must cover the points not covered by non-sunny lines. But we can also have non-sunny lines covering many points. The necessary condition is that the complement after (n-k) non-sunny lines must be coverable by k sunny lines. The maximum number of points that (n-k) non-sunny lines can cover is something like sum of the largest (n-k) row sizes or column sizes, but we need to see if we can achieve a configuration with k>3 for n≥4. For n=4, k=4 is impossible as argued. For n=5, k=4? That would be 4 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. With 1 non-sunny line, max coverage is 5 (row b=1). Complement 10 points. 4 sunny lines can cover at most 4 * M where M is max per sunny line. For n=5, M is 3 (from y=x). So 4*3=12 >10, so not ruled out. But can we actually construct? We can try to use the same idea: take 1 non-sunny line (say, y=1) covering 5 points. Complement = points with b≥2, which are 10 points. Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover those 10 points. Is that possible? Let's examine S_5 complement after y=1: points with b=2,3,4,5 (10 points). That's a set of 10 points. Can we cover them with 4 sunny lines? The maximum per sunny line is 3. 4*3=12, so maybe. But we need to construct explicitly. We can attempt to use lines like:

L1: y = x (covers (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but only (2,2),(3,3) are in complement? (2,2) and (3,3) are in complement (b=2,3). That's 2.
L2: y = 2x (covers (1,2),(2,4) — (2,4) is in complement (b=4). That's 2.
L3: y = -2x + 8? That covers (2,4) and (3,2) but (3,2) is in complement? (3,2) is b=2, yes. That's 2.
L4: y = -2x + 7 covers (1,5),(2,3),(3,1) — (1,5) and (2,3) are in complement. That's 2.
We have 4 lines covering 2+2+2+2 = 8 points, but we need 10. So we need some lines with 3 points. Are there any sunny lines that contain 3 points from this complement? Let's see. The complement is all points with b≥2. The points are:

b=2: a=1..4 → (1,2),(2,2),(3,2),(4,2)
b=3: a=1..3 → (1,3),(2,3),(3,3)
b=4: a=1..2 → (1,4),(2,4)
b=5: a=1 → (1,5)

That's 10 points. Are there any lines with slope not 0,∞,-1 that contain three of these points? For example, line y = x (slope 1) contains (1,1) not, (2,2) (in complement), (3,3) (in complement). That's 2 points. (4,4) not in S_5. So 2. Slope 2: (1,2) and (2,4) gives 2. Slope -2: (2,4) and (3,2) gives 2. Slope -1/2: (1,3) and (3,2) gives 2. So max appears to be 2. So with 4 sunny lines, at most 8 points can be covered, insufficient. Therefore k=4 impossible for n=5. So indeed, for n≥4, k>3 is impossible. That seems plausible.

Now, we should prove that for n≥3, the only possible k are 0, 1, 3. But we also need to consider n=3: we have k=0,1,3. That's the set. For n=4,5, we have 0,1,3. For n≥6, we also have 0,1,3. So the answer is: For all n≥3, k can be 0, 1, 3. But is k=3 possible for n=3? Yes. So overall, the achievable k are 0, 1, 3, regardless of n (n≥3). However we must also confirm that for n=3, k=2 is impossible, and also k=0,1,3 are possible. That matches.

But we should also check if for n=3, k=3 is possible. We gave an example: L1: y=x, L2: y=2x-1, L3: y=2x-3? Actually we had L1: y=x, L2: through (1,2) and (3,1) (slope -1/2), L3: through (1,3) and (2,1) (slope -2). That covers S_3. So k=3.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous solution. The solution should:

- Define S_n.
- Show that k=0 is possible.
- Show that k=1 is possible.
- Show that k=3 is possible (for n≥3). Provide a construction.
- Show that k=2 is impossible.
- Show that k>3 is impossible.
- Conclude that the possible k are exactly 0, 1, 3 for any n≥3.

But careful: The problem says "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:" So we need to characterize k in terms of n. The answer might be: all k with 0 ≤ k ≤ n, except k = 2, and also for n = 3, k = 4,5,6 are impossible (obviously). But we have shown that k=3 is possible for all n≥3. So the set is {0,1,3} for all n≥3. But is k=3 possible for n=3? Yes. For n=4,5,6,... also yes. So that seems to be the answer. However we need to double-check for n=3, k=3 is possible. For n=4, we gave a construction with 1 non-sunny line (y=1) and 3 sunny lines. But does that configuration satisfy that exactly 3 of the n lines are sunny? Yes, because we have 1 non-sunny and 3 sunny, total 4 lines. That's k=3. So it's valid. For n=5, we gave a construction with 2 non-sunny lines (y=1, y=2) and 3 sunny lines. That's 5 lines, with 3 sunny. That's valid. So indeed k=3 is possible for n≥3. So the answer is independent of n.

Now, we need to prove that k=2 is impossible. Let's attempt a proof.

We have n lines. Suppose exactly k of them are sunny. Then n-k lines are not sunny, i.e., they are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. We need to cover S_n.

Idea: Show that any line that is not sunny can contain at most something like n points (obviously). But we need a contradiction if k=2.

We can try to bound the total number of points that can be covered by the n lines, using the fact that any line not sunny cannot cover points that are on a line of forbidden direction, but we need to consider coverage.

Alternatively, we can use a combinatorial argument: For n lines, consider the set of points that lie on at least one line. We need to cover all points. The total number of points is n(n+1)/2. If we have only 2 sunny lines, then the other n-2 lines are non-sunny. The maximum number of points that n-2 non-sunny lines can cover is something like the sum of the sizes of the largest n-2 rows (or columns or anti-diagonals). But we need to show that even with the best possible selection, the complement size is > 2 * (max points per sunny line), making it impossible for 2 sunny lines to cover it. However we need to be careful: The complement size after non-sunny lines is the number of points not covered by those lines. The sunny lines can cover those points, but they may also cover points already covered by non-sunny lines. The condition is that all points must be covered by at least one of the n lines. So if the non-sunny lines cover U points, then the sunny lines must cover the remaining |S_n| - U points (since they can cover points already covered by non-sunny lines as well, but that doesn't help cover uncovered points). So the set of uncovered points (after considering non-sunny lines) must be covered by sunny lines. Thus we need that the number of uncovered points is ≤ (number of sunny lines) * (maximum number of points that a sunny line can contain from S_n). However this is only an upper bound; we need a lower bound on uncovered points given n-2 non-sunny lines, to show it exceeds 2 * M. But we also need to consider that we can choose which non-sunny lines to use. The best we can do to minimize uncovered points is to choose non-sunny lines that cover as many points as possible. So we need to find the minimum possible uncovered points after selecting n-2 non-sunny lines. That minimum is |S_n| - max_{set of n-2 non-sunny lines} |U|. Then if that minimum > 2 * M, then k=2 impossible. But we also need to check if it's possible that uncovered points are covered by 2 sunny lines even if they exceed 2 * M? No, because a sunny line can contain at most M points from S_n. So if uncovered points > 2M, then it's impossible. But uncovered points could be ≤ 2M, but maybe still impossible due to geometry. So we need to bound max U. Let's compute max U for m non-sunny lines.

We have three types of lines: horizontal (H), vertical (V), anti-diagonal (D). Each line is a straight line. The maximum union size of m such lines is achieved by picking the m lines that are as disjoint as possible. Since rows are disjoint, we could take the m largest rows. Similarly, columns are disjoint. Anti-diagonals are disjoint. So the maximum union size for m lines is at least sum of the m largest row sizes. But can we get larger union by mixing types? For example, using a horizontal line and a vertical line, they intersect, so not disjoint. The union size is less than sum of their sizes. So to maximize union, we want lines that are pairwise disjoint, i.e., no overlap. Rows are disjoint. Columns are disjoint. Anti-diagonals are disjoint. So the maximum union is achieved by taking a set of m pairwise disjoint lines from the collection of all H,V,D. Since H lines are disjoint among themselves, we can take the m largest rows. That gives sum_{b=1}^{m} (n+1-b) = m(2n+3-m)/2? Actually sum_{b=1}^{m} (n+1-b) = m(n+1) - m(m+1)/2. That's a quadratic. Similarly, columns give same. Anti-diagonals give sum_{c=2}^{m+1} (c-1) = m(m+1)/2? Actually anti-diagonals for c=2..m+1 have sizes 1,2,...,m, sum = m(m+1)/2. But the maximum per line is n (for c=n+1). So taking the m largest anti-diagonals (c=n+1 down to n+2-m) yields sizes n, n-1, ..., n-m+1, sum = m(2n-m+1)/2. That's larger than rows? Compare: For m=2, rows sum: 2*(n+1) - 3 = 2n+2-3=2n-1. Anti-diagonals sum: n + (n-1) = 2n-1. So equal. For m=3, rows sum: 3(n+1) - 6 = 3n+3-6 = 3n-3. Anti-diagonals sum: n + (n-1) + (n-2) = 3n-3. So equal. In general, the sum of the m largest rows (b=1..m) is m(n+1) - m(m+1)/2. The sum of the m largest anti-diagonals (c=n+1 down to n+2-m) is m(2n-m+1)/2. These are equal? Let's check: m(n+1) - m(m+1)/2 = (2m(n+1) - m(m+1))/2 = (2mn + 2m - m^2 - m)/2 = (2mn + m - m^2)/2 = m(2n + 1 - m)/2. That's m(2n+1-m)/2. The anti-diagonal sum is m(2n-m+1)/2. Same. So indeed, the sum of the m largest rows equals the sum of the m largest anti-diagonals (since the largest anti-diagonal has size n, then n-1, etc., and the largest rows have size n, n-1, etc., because row b=1 has size n, b=2 has n-1, etc., and anti-diagonal c=n+1 has size n, c=n has size n-1, etc.). So both give same bound. So the maximum union size with m non-sunny lines is at least that sum. But could we get a larger union by using some combination that is not all rows or all anti-diagonals? Since rows and anti-diagonals are disjoint, we could also take some rows and some anti-diagonals that are disjoint from each other, but they might intersect. However to maximize union, we would like to avoid overlaps. Since rows and anti-diagonals intersect, we cannot simply take a mix without overlap. But we can consider taking m lines that are all of the same type (rows) and that seems to give a large union. Is it possible that using a mix yields a larger union than taking the m largest rows? For example, suppose we take m-1 rows and 1 anti-diagonal that is disjoint from those rows. But anti-diagonals intersect rows. However there might be some anti-diagonals that are disjoint from certain rows? For a row b, the anti-diagonal with c = a+b. Overlap occurs if a+b = c and b is within row. For a fixed anti-diagonal, it intersects each row at most once. So taking an anti-diagonal reduces the union because it overlaps with some rows. So it's better to take all rows. So the maximum union is achieved by taking the m largest rows, or equivalently the m largest anti-diagonals. So the maximum union size U_max(m) = m(2n+1-m)/2.

Now, we have m = n-2 non-sunny lines. Then U_max = (n-2)(2n+1-(n-2))/2 = (n-2)(n+3)/2. That's the maximum number of points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines. But is it always achievable? We can take rows b=1..n-2, that's n-2 rows, covering that many points. So yes. So with n-2 non-sunny lines, the maximum union is at least that. But the actual union U could be less than that if we choose a different set. The minimum possible uncovered points after selecting n-2 non-sunny lines is |S_n| - U_max, because we can choose the best non-sunny lines to cover as many as possible. So we have |S_n| - U_max = n(n+1)/2 - (n-2)(n+3)/2 = [n(n+1) - (n-2)(n+3)]/2.

Compute:

n(n+1) = n^2 + n.
(n-2)(n+3) = n^2 + 3n - 2n -6 = n^2 + n -6.
Difference = (n^2 + n) - (n^2 + n -6) = 6.
So |S_n| - U_max = 6/2 = 3. That's interesting: the complement size after the best possible selection of n-2 non-sunny lines is 3. That's the minimum possible uncovered points. So with n-2 non-sunny lines, we can cover all but 3 points. That's the best we can do. Then we have 2 sunny lines to cover those 3 points. So we need to check whether it's possible to cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines. As we saw, the 3 uncovered points are the points not covered by the rows b=1..(n-2). Those are the points with b≥n-1 (i.e., b = n-1, n, and also maybe b=n-2? Wait rows 1..n-2 cover b=1..n-2. The remaining points are those with b≥n-1. That's rows n-1 and n. But earlier we computed complement size as 3. However rows n-1 and n have sizes: row n-1 has 2 points (since a ≤ n+1 - (n-1) = 2), row n has 1 point. That's 3 points. So indeed, the uncovered points are: (1,n-1),(2,n-1) and (1,n). But wait we also have row n-2? That's covered. So the three points are as described. So we need to see if those three points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. As we saw, any line passing through two of these points is non-sunny. So each sunny line can contain at most 1 of these points. Thus with 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points can be covered, not 3. Therefore k=2 impossible. This argument is rigorous.

Now, we need to also consider the possibility that we don't take the best possible non-sunny lines; we could take a different set of n-2 lines that might leave a complement of size >3. That would be even worse. So if we can't cover the minimum complement (size 3) with 2 sunny lines, then certainly any configuration with 2 sunny lines cannot cover all points, because the uncovered points after non-sunny lines will be at least 3. But is it possible that with a different selection of non-sunny lines, the complement size is 2 or 1, making it easier? The complement size after n-2 non-sunny lines is at least |S_n| - U_max. So we need to examine the possible triples of points that are the complement of a covering by two non-sunny lines. The maximum coverage by two non-sunny lines is 7, but maybe we can choose two non-sunny lines that cover exactly 7 points, leaving a specific triple. Then we need to check if that triple can be covered by two sunny lines. If we can find a triple where two points are collinear on a sunny line, then that triple can be covered by two sunny lines (one line covering the pair, the other covering the third point). But is it possible that the triple is such that any pair is non-sunny? Let's analyze.

We need to find a pair of non-sunny lines (type horizontal, vertical, slope -1) that cover 7 points. That seems to be the maximum. But which triples are left? Let's compute complement for each combination.

Alternate perspective: The problem is from combinatorial geometry. The question: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:" So we need to characterize k as a function of n. Therefore we need a set of 4 non-sunny lines whose union covers at least 21 - 4 = 17 points. So we need union size ≥ 17. The maximum is 18, so we can aim for 17 or 18. If we get 18, complement 3; if 17, complement 4. Both are ≤ 4, so potentially coverable by 2 sunny lines if the set of uncovered points is such that 2 sunny lines can cover them. But we also need the sunny lines to be distinct and sunny.

Now, we need to examine if there exists a quadruple of non-sunny lines covering 17 or 18 points such that the uncovered points (size 3 or 4) can be covered by 2 sunny lines. For n=6, we can try to construct.

First, note that the points in S_6 have coordinates a,b≥1,a+b≤7. The points can be organized in a triangular array. The rows (b constant) and columns (a constant) are lines of forbidden types. The anti-diagonals (a+b constant) are also forbidden. So non-sunny lines are exactly those.

We want to cover all points with 4 non-sunny lines and 2 sunny lines. Since we have many lines, we can try to use a combination like:

- Three horizontals H1,H2,H3 and one anti-diagonal D7? That's 4 lines, union size 18, complement 3 points: (1,4),(2,4),(1,5). That's also not coverable.

- Four verticals V1,V2,V3,V4 cover 18 points, complement 3 points: (5,1),(5,2),(6,1). Not coverable.

Overlaps are pairwise. However we also have possible triple overlaps. But the union size can be at most 21. Let's compute systematically.

Given S_n, we can consider the set of points with a+b = n+1 (the antidiagonal). Those are n points, not sunny. The points with a+b ≤ n are covered by some lines. Thus seems all triples that appear as complements of 4 non-sunny lines (with union size 18) are such that all pairs are on forbidden lines. That suggests that k=2 is impossible for n=6 as well. That would be even more restrictive, but maybe possible. However the union size cannot be 17? Let's see if any combination of 4 non-sunny lines covers exactly 17 points. For n=6, the maximum is 18, but maybe some combination gives 17. For example, H1,H2,H3,V1 gave union 18. H1,H2,H3,V2 gave 18? Let's try H1,H2,H3,V2. That's 4 lines. Compute union size. H1 (6), H2 (5), H3 (4) → 15. V2 (5) → overlaps: V2 with H1 at (2,1), with H2 at (2,2), with H3 at (2,3). So union = 15+5-3 = 17? Actually H1∪H2∪H3 = 15 distinct points. So union size = 15+2 = 17. So that's a quadruple with union size 17. Complement = 4 points. Which points are not covered? Let's list S6 points and see which are not in H1∪H2∪H3∪V2. H1∪H2∪H3 covers all points with b≤3. V2 covers a=2 points (2,1)..(2,5). So the uncovered points after H1..H3 are those with b≥4 (i.e., points with b=4,5,6). They are: (1,4),(2,4),(3,4),(1,5),(2,5),(1,6). That's 6 points. Adding V2 covers (2,4) and (2,5) from that set. So remaining uncovered are (1,4),(3,4),(1,5),(1,6). That's 4 points. So T = {(1,4),(3,4),(1,5),(1,6)}. Now, can we cover these 4 points with 2 sunny lines? Let's see. (1,4) and (3,4) are on horizontal y=4, not sunny. (1,4) and (1,5) vertical, not sunny. (1,4) and (1,6) vertical. (3,4) and (1,5) slope (5-4)/(1-3)=1/(-2) = -1/2, sunny? That's a line with slope -1/2, but does it pass through (3,4) and (1,5)? Yes. That line is y = -1/2 x + 7/2? Let's compute: through (1,5) slope -1/2 → y-5 = -1/2(x-1) → y = -1/2 x + 1/2 +5 = -1/2 x + 5.5. At x=3, y = -1.5+5.5=4, so it passes through (3,4). So that line is sunny. So it covers (1,5) and (3,4). Similarly, (1,4) and (1,6) are vertical, not sunny. But maybe we can cover (1,4) and (1,5) with a line of slope? They are vertical, so not sunny. (1,4) and (3,4) horizontal. So to cover all four points with two sunny lines, we need to find two sunny lines such that each point lies on at least one. Since we have 4 points, we can try to cover (1,5) and (3,4) with L1 (slope -1/2). Then we need to cover (1,4) and (1,6) with L2. But (1,4) and (1,6) are vertical, so L2 would need to be vertical, but vertical lines are not sunny. However we could cover (1,4) with a sunny line that is not vertical, e.g., a line through (1,4) with some slope, and similarly cover (1,6) with another sunny line. But we only have 2 lines. If L1 covers (1,5) and (3,4), L2 can cover (1,4) and (1,6) only if L2 is a line that passes through both (1,4) and (1,6) (which is vertical) or passes through each individually but not both. L2 could be a line that passes through (1,4) and also passes through some other point, but it doesn't need to pass through (1,6). We can have L2 cover (1,4) and also maybe (1,6) if it passes through both; but if it doesn't, then (1,6) would be uncovered. So we need to cover (1,4) and (1,6) with the two sunny lines. But we already used one sunny line (L1) covering two points. The second sunny line can be any line (sunny). It could be a line through (1,4) with some slope, and then we would need to also cover (1,6). But if L2 is a line that does not pass through (1,6), then (1,6) would be uncovered. So we need a line that passes through both (1,4) and (1,6). That's vertical, not sunny. So not allowed. Alternatively, we could try a different combination of sunny lines: perhaps one line covers three points? But we need to cover 4 points. With 2 sunny lines, the maximum number of distinct points they can cover is at most 2 each (if each has 2 points). So 4 points is possible if each sunny line covers exactly 2 points, and the sets are disjoint. In this T, we have points (1,4),(3,4),(1,5),(1,6). Can we partition them into two pairs, each pair lying on a sunny line? The pairs:

- (1,4) and (3,4): horizontal, not sunny.
- (1,4) and (1,5): vertical, not sunny.
- (1,4) and (1,6): vertical, not sunny.
- (3,4) and (1,5): slope -1/2, sunny.
- (3,4) and (1,6): slope (6-4)/(1-3) = 2/(-2) = -1, not sunny.
- (1,5) and (1,6): vertical, not sunny.

So only one sunny pair exists: (3,4) and (1,5). That's one pair. The other two points (1,4) and (1,6) are not a sunny pair. So with 2 sunny lines, we cannot cover all four points. Could we use a line that covers (1,4) and also (3,4) but that's not sunny. So indeed, impossible. Thus k=2 is impossible for n=6.

Now, what about k=3 for n=6? We already argued impossible. So for n=6, the only possible k are 0 and 1. But is k=0 possible? Yes, with 6 non-sunny lines covering S_6, e.g., all anti-diagonals D3..D7 (5 lines) but we need 6 lines. We can use D3..D7 (5 lines) and then add one more non-sunny line (like H1 or V1) that is redundant. That would give k=0, but we need exactly n lines. So we can take the 5 anti-diagonals covering all points, and then add a sixth line that is also non-sunny (like another anti-diagonal D8, but D8 has no points, but we can still include it as a line; it's allowed because lines are just lines, they don't have to intersect S. However the condition "exactly k of the n lines are sunny" means that among the n lines, exactly k are sunny. If we include a line that is not sunny, then it's fine. So we can take the 5 anti-diagonals (non-sunny) covering S_6, and then add any non-sunny line (like x=0, or y=0, or D8) that is not sunny. That gives a configuration with 6 lines, all non-sunny, k=0. That's valid. So k=0 is possible.

But is there a configuration with k=1 for n=6? Yes, we can use 5 non-sunny lines covering S_6 and add one sunny line (redundant). That's k=1. So k=1 is possible. So for n=6, possible k are 0,1. That's a set of size 2. For n=3,4,5, possible k are 0,1,3. So there is a transition. For n=7, we need to check.

The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:" So we need to give an answer based on n. It seems the answer might be: for n=3, k∈{0,1,3}; for n=4,5, k∈{0,1,3}; for n≥6, k∈{0,1} (or maybe also k=2 for some n?). But we need to prove that for n≥6, k=2 and k=3 are impossible. Also check if k=n is impossible for n≥4, which we already argued. So the possible k are 0,1 for n≥6, but also 3 for n=4,5. That's interesting. But we also need to consider n=3,4,5 where 3 is possible. So the answer may be:

- If n = 3, then k can be 0, 1, 3.
- If n = 4 or 5, then k can be 0, 1, 3.
- If n ≥ 6, then k can be 0, 1.

But is k=2 possible for any n? We suspect not, but we need to prove it in general. Let's analyze the geometry more abstractly.

We have S_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a≥1, b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1}. The lines are in the plane. A line is sunny if it is not parallel to any of the lines: x-axis (slope 0), y-axis (infinite slope), x+y=0 (slope -1). So sunny lines have slopes m ∈ ℝ ∪ {∞} such that m ∉ {0, ∞, -1} (and m ≠ ∞ for vertical lines? Actually vertical lines have slope ∞, so they are not sunny). So sunny lines have slopes not equal to 0, ∞, -1.

Now, we need to find, for a given n, which k are achievable. The examples:

- k=0: take n horizontal lines y = 1,2,…,n. These are all non-sunny. Do they cover S_n? For any point (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1, b ≤ n, so it is covered by y=b. Yes. So k=0 is possible.

- k=1: take n-1 horizontal lines (y=1,…,n-1) and one sunny line, e.g., y = x (slope 1) that covers the point (1,n). But we need to ensure that all points with b=n are covered; the points with b=n are (1,n) only (since a≥1, a+b ≤ n+1 → a ≤ n+1 - n = 1). So (1,n) is covered by y=n (non-sunny). So to have exactly 1 sunny line, we could use all horizontals except one, and then replace that one with a sunny line that covers the points not covered by the other horizontals? Actually if we use horizontals y=1,…,n-1, then points with b=n (which is (1,n)) are not covered, so we need to cover it. We can add a sunny line that passes through (1,n) and is sunny. That's one sunny line. But we have n lines total; we used n-1 horizontals and 1 sunny line, total n lines. That's valid. So k=1 is possible.

- k=n: all lines sunny. For n=3, we gave an example. For n≥4, we need to see if it's possible. As argued, with n sunny lines, each line can contain at most 3 points from S_n? Actually the maximum number of points from S_n on a sunny line depends on n. For n≥4, we saw that there exist sunny lines with 3 points. For n≥6, there are sunny lines with 3 points. But can we have n sunny lines covering all points? The total points is n(n+1)/2. If we have n lines, each can cover at most some number. The maximum coverage per line is something like the size of the largest collinear subset of S_n that is not parallel to forbidden directions. But we need to compute an upper bound.

Let's try to derive a general bound. For a given n, consider the set of points (a,b) with a+b = c (c ≤ n+1). The size of that anti-diagonal is c-1. The anti-diagonal with c = n+1 has size n. That's a set of points all on a line of slope -1, which is not sunny. So any sunny line cannot contain all those points. But it could contain some of them. However, due to the structure, it's plausible that a sunny line can contain at most n points? That's not helpful. That's about n/2. For slope -2, it's about n/2 as well. For slope -1/2, it's about n/2. So at most about n/2. Actually we can get up to n points? For n=6, we got 3 points on slope -2. That's n/2. For n=7, we might get 3 or 4? Let's compute. For n=7, S_7 has 28 points. The anti-diagonal a+b=8 has 7 points. Sunny lines could have up to 3 points maybe. For n=5, we had a line with 3 points. So the maximum number of points on a sunny line in S_n is:

- If n ≤ 3, it's at most 2.
- If n ≥ 4, it's at most 3? Actually for n=4, we found a line with 3 points (y=-2x+7). That's 3 points. So indeed for n≥4, there exist sunny lines with 3 points. For n=3, max is 2. Let's try to find a line with slope, say, -1/2 that passes through many integer points with a+b ≤ n+1. Let's think about the problem more deeply. We have n lines, they can be chosen arbitrarily. The condition is that for all (a,b) with a+b ≤ n+1, there exists at least one line among the n that passes through that point. So we need to cover a triangular set of lattice points.

The lines are in the plane. The non-sunny lines are those that are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. So they are "special". The sunny lines are all others.

We are to determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines with exactly k sunny lines covering S_n.

Observation: If we can cover S_n with some lines, we can add extra lines (sunny or not) to increase the count of lines, but we need exactly n lines. So we need to find a set of n lines (distinct) that covers S_n. The sunny count is the number among them that are sunny.

The problem asks to "determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both conditions." So for each n, we need to give a set of k (depending on n). The answer might be:

That is a combinatorial geometry problem.

Let's define the triangular grid. We have points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b ≤ n+1. We want to find the maximum number of such points that can lie on a line (with slope m ∉ {0, ∞, -1}). This is like finding the maximum number of lattice points in a triangular region on a line.

Observations: The lines that contain many points are those with rational slopes. The region is a right triangle. The maximum collinear points will be achieved by lines that are "diagonals" of a grid, but we cannot use slope -1. So we need to analyze.

We can think of the points as integer lattice points in the triangle. The line with slope 1 contains points where a=b. The number of such points is floor((n+1)/2). That's about n/2. The line with slope -2 contains points where b = -2a + c. For integer a, b is integer when c is integer. The points are (a, -2a + c). To have a,b≥1 and a+b ≤ n+1. The number of such points is about n/3 maybe. For n=6, we got 3 points with slope -2. For n=7, slope -2 gives (1,6) sum7? Actually a+b ≤8, so (1,6) sum7, (2,4) sum6, (3,2) sum5, (4,0) no. That's 3 points. So that's less than 4. So the maximum per line seems to be floor((n+1)/2) from y=x for n≥? But for n=4, floor((4+1)/2)=2, but we have a line with 3 points. So the maximum can exceed floor((n+1)/2) for small n. For n=4, we have 3 points on slope -2. For n=5, floor((5+1)/2)=3, and we have 3 points on y=x and also on y=-2x+7. So M=3. For n=6, floor((6+1)/2)=3, M=3. For n=7, floor((7+1)/2)=4, but maybe there exists a line with 4 points? y=x has 4. Also slope -2 gives 3. So M=4. So M = max(floor((n+1)/2), something else). But for n≥4, the line y=x gives floor((n+1)/2). For n=4, that's 2, but we have 3. So the maximum is larger for n=4. So M is not monotonic in n? Actually for n=4, M=3; for n=5, M=3; for n=6, M=3; for n=7, M=4; so M increases.

Thus to determine possible k, we need to consider the geometry.

The problem might have a known solution: the possible k are all integers from 0 to n, except that k = n-1 and k = n are impossible for n ≥ 6? Possibly one line covers (1,4) and (3,4) is horizontal (not sunny). (1,4) and (1,5) vertical, not. (1,4) and (1,6) vertical, not. (3,4) and (1,5) are on a sunny line (slope -1/2). Plug x=1, y=4 => 4 = -1/2*1 + c → c = 4.5, so line y = -1/2 x + 4.5. That passes through (1,4) and (3,4) (since -1.5+4.5=3). But (3,4) is in T, so that line would cover (1,4) and (3,4). However the points (1,4) and (3,4) are on the horizontal line y=4. But the line through (1,4) and (3,4) is indeed horizontal (since they have same y). So any line passing through both (1,4) and (3,4) must be horizontal, because it's a straight line. That's a line with slope 0. So that line is not sunny. So L1 with slope -1/2 cannot pass through both (1,4) and (3,4) because that would require it to be horizontal. So my earlier statement about line through (1,4) and (3,4) being horizontal is correct. So L1 cannot be both slope -1/2 and pass through both. So the only sunny line that can cover (1,4) and (3,4) is impossible. So indeed, the complement T we got from H1+H2+H3+V2 is not coverable by 2 sunny lines. So that quadruple fails.

The problem is to "determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following". So for each n, we need to characterize the set of achievable k. The examples:

- n=3: k=0,1,3 are achievable.
- n=4: k=0,1,3 are achievable.
- n=5: k=0,1,3 are achievable.
- n=6: we found k=0,1,3 are achievable (k=3 with rows 1..3 and 3 sunny lines). Also k=2? Not achievable. k=4,5,6? Not achievable.
- n=7: we found k=0,1,3 are achievable (k=3 with rows 1..4 and 3 sunny lines). Could k=2 be achievable? We attempted and it seemed not. But we should test with a construction for n=7, k=2 using maybe 5 non-sunny lines that cover 25 points, leaving 3 points; but those 3 points are not coverable. Could we use 5 non-sunny lines that cover 24 points, leaving 4 points, and then 2 sunny lines covering those 4 points? That would require the 4 points to be coverable by 2 sunny lines. Let's examine the complement for a set of 5 non-sunny lines that are, say, rows b=1,2,3,4,6 (size 7+6+5+4+2=24). Complement is rows b=5 (size 3) and b=7 (size 1) → 4 points: (1,5),(2,5),(3,5),(1,7). Now, we need to produce a rigorous solution. The solution should:

- Define S_n.
- Show that k=0 is possible.
- Show that k=1 is possible.
- Show that k=3 is possible (for n≥3). Provide a construction.
- Show that k=2 is impossible.
- Show that k>3 is impossible.
- Conclude that the possible k are exactly 0, 1, 3 for any n≥3.

But careful: The problem says "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following:" So we need to characterize k in terms of n. The answer might be: all k with 0 ≤ k ≤ n, except k = 2, and also for n = 3, k = 4,5,6 are impossible (obviously). But we have shown that k=3 is possible for all n≥3. So the set is {0,1,3} for all n≥3. But is k=3 possible for n=3? Yes. For n=4,5,6,... also yes. So that seems to be the answer. However we need to double-check for n=3, k=3 is possible. For n=4, we gave a construction with 1 non-sunny line (y=1) and 3 sunny lines. But does that configuration satisfy that exactly 3 of the n lines are sunny? Yes, because we have 1 non-sunny and 3 sunny, total 4 lines. That's k=3. So it's valid. For n=5, we gave a construction with 2 non-sunny lines (y=1, y=2) and 3 sunny lines. That's 5 lines, with 3 sunny. That's valid. So indeed k=3 is possible for n≥3. So the answer is independent of n.

Now, we need to prove that k=2 is impossible. Let's attempt a proof.

We have n lines. Suppose exactly k of them are sunny. Then n-k lines are not sunny, i.e., they are horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. We need to cover S_n.

Idea: Show that any line that is not sunny can contain at most something like n points (obviously). But we need a contradiction if k=2.

We can try to bound the total number of points that can be covered by the n lines, using the fact that any line not sunny cannot cover points that are on a line of forbidden direction, but we need to consider coverage.

Alternatively, we can use a combinatorial argument: For n lines, consider the set of points that lie on at least one line. We need to cover all points. The total number of points is n(n+1)/2. If we have only 2 sunny lines, then the other n-2 lines are non-sunny. The maximum number of points that n-2 non-sunny lines can cover is something like the sum of the sizes of the largest n-2 rows (or columns or anti-diagonals). But we need to show that even with the best possible selection, the complement size is > 2 * (max points per sunny line), making it impossible for 2 sunny lines to cover it. However we need to be careful: The complement size after non-sunny lines is the number of points not covered by those lines. The sunny lines can cover those points, but they may also cover points already covered by non-sunny lines. The condition is that all points must be covered by at least one of the n lines. So if the non-sunny lines cover U points, then the sunny lines must cover the remaining |S_n| - U points (since they can cover points already covered by non-sunny lines as well, but that doesn't help cover uncovered points). So the set of uncovered points (after considering non-sunny lines) must be covered by sunny lines. Thus we need that the number of uncovered points is ≤ (number of sunny lines) * (maximum number of points that a sunny line can contain from S_n). However this is only an upper bound; we need a lower bound on uncovered points given n-2 non-sunny lines, to show it exceeds 2 * M. But we also need to consider that we can choose which non-sunny lines to use. The best we can do to minimize uncovered points is to choose non-sunny lines that cover as many points as possible. So we need to find the minimum possible uncovered points after selecting n-2 non-sunny lines. That minimum is |S_n| - max_{set of n-2 non-sunny lines} |U|. Then if that minimum > 2 * M, then k=2 impossible. But we also need to check if it's possible that uncovered points are covered by 2 sunny lines even if they exceed 2 * M? No, because a sunny line can contain at most M points from S_n. So if uncovered points > 2M, then it's impossible. But uncovered points could be ≤ 2M, but maybe still impossible due to geometry. So we need to bound max U. Let's compute max U for m non-sunny lines.

We have three types of lines: horizontal (H), vertical (V), anti-diagonal (D). Each line is a straight line. The maximum union size of m such lines is achieved by picking the m lines that are as disjoint as possible. Since rows are disjoint, we could take the m largest rows. Similarly, columns are disjoint. Anti-diagonals are disjoint. So the maximum union size for m lines is at least sum of the m largest row sizes. But can we get larger union by mixing types? For example, using a horizontal line and a vertical line, they intersect, so not disjoint. The union size is less than sum of their sizes. So to maximize union, we want lines that are pairwise disjoint, i.e., no overlap. Rows are disjoint. Columns are disjoint. Anti-diagonals are disjoint. So the maximum union is achieved by taking a set of m pairwise disjoint lines from the collection of all H,V,D. Since H lines are disjoint among themselves, we can take the m largest rows. That gives sum_{b=1}^{m} (n+1-b) = m(2n+3-m)/2? Actually sum_{b=1}^{m} (n+1-b) = m(n+1) - m(m+1)/2. That's a quadratic. Similarly, columns give same. Anti-diagonals give sum_{c=2}^{m+1} (c-1) = m(m+1)/2? Actually anti-diagonals for c=2..m+1 have sizes 1,2,...,m, sum = m(m+1)/2. But the maximum per line is n (for c=n+1). So taking the m largest anti-diagonals (c=n+1 down to n+2-m) yields sizes n, n-1, ..., n-m+1, sum = m(2n-m+1)/2. That's larger than rows? Compare: For m=2, rows sum: 2*(n+1) - 3 = 2n+2-3=2n-1. Anti-diagonals sum: n + (n-1) = 2n-1. So equal. For m=3, rows sum: 3(n+1) - 6 = 3n+3-6 = 3n-3. Anti-diagonals sum: n + (n-1) + (n-2) = 3n-3. So equal. In general, the sum of the m largest rows (b=1..m) is m(n+1) - m(m+1)/2. The sum of the m largest anti-diagonals (c=n+1 down to n+2-m) is m(2n-m+1)/2. These are equal? Let's check: m(n+1) - m(m+1)/2 = (2m(n+1) - m(m+1))/2 = (2mn + 2m - m^2 - m)/2 = (2mn + m - m^2)/2 = m(2n + 1 - m)/2. That's m(2n+1-m)/2. The anti-diagonal sum is m(2n-m+1)/2. Same. So indeed, the sum of the m largest rows equals the sum of the m largest anti-diagonals (since the largest anti-diagonal has size n, then n-1, etc., and the largest rows have size n, n-1, etc., because row b=1 has size n, b=2 has n-1, etc., and anti-diagonal c=n+1 has size n, c=n has size n-1, etc.). So both give same bound. So the maximum union size with m non-sunny lines is at least that sum. But could we get a larger union by using some combination that is not all rows or all anti-diagonals? Since rows and anti-diagonals are disjoint, we could also take some rows and some anti-diagonals that are disjoint from each other, but they might intersect. However to maximize union, we would like to avoid overlaps. Since rows and anti-diagonals intersect, we cannot simply take a mix without overlap. But we can consider taking m lines that are all of the same type (rows) and that seems to give a large union. Is it possible that using a mix yields a larger union than taking the m largest rows? For example, suppose we take m-1 rows and 1 anti-diagonal that is disjoint from those rows. But anti-diagonals intersect rows. However there might be some anti-diagonals that are disjoint from certain rows? For a row b, the anti-diagonal with c = a+b. Overlap occurs if a+b = c and b is within row. For a fixed anti-diagonal, it intersects each row at most once. So taking an anti-diagonal reduces the union because it overlaps with some rows. So it's better to take all rows. So the maximum union is achieved by taking the m largest rows, or equivalently the m largest anti-diagonals. So the maximum union size U_max(m) = m(2n+1-m)/2.

Now, we have m = n-2 non-sunny lines. Then U_max = (n-2)(2n+1-(n-2))/2 = (n-2)(n+3)/2. That's the maximum number of points that can be covered by n-2 non-sunny lines. But is it always achievable? We can take rows b=1..n-2, that's n-2 rows, covering that many points. So yes. So with n-2 non-sunny lines, the maximum union is at least that. But the actual union U could be less than that if we choose a different set. The minimum possible uncovered points after selecting n-2 non-sunny lines is |S_n| - U_max, because we can choose the best non-sunny lines to cover as many as possible. So we have |S_n| - U_max = n(n+1)/2 - (n-2)(n+3)/2 = [n(n+1) - (n-2)(n+3)]/2.

Compute:

n(n+1) = n^2 + n.
(n-2)(n+3) = n^2 + 3n - 2n -6 = n^2 + n -6.
Difference = (n^2 + n) - (n^2 + n -6) = 6.
So |S_n| - U_max = 6/2 = 3. That's interesting: the complement size after the best possible selection of n-2 non-sunny lines is 3. That's the minimum possible uncovered points. So with n-2 non-sunny lines, we can cover all but 3 points. That's the best we can do. Then we have 2 sunny lines to cover those 3 points. So we need to check whether it's possible to cover those 3 points with 2 sunny lines. As we saw, the 3 uncovered points are the points not covered by the rows b=1..(n-2). Those are the points with b≥n-1 (i.e., b = n-1, n, and also maybe b=n-2? Wait rows 1..n-2 cover b=1..n-2. The remaining points are those with b≥n-1. That's rows n-1 and n. But earlier we computed complement size as 3. However rows n-1 and n have sizes: row n-1 has 2 points (since a ≤ n+1 - (n-1) = 2), row n has 1 point. That's 3 points. So indeed, the uncovered points are: (1,n-1),(2,n-1) and (1,n). But wait we also have row n-2? That's covered. So the three points are as described. So we need to see if those three points can be covered by 2 sunny lines. As we saw, any line passing through two of these points is non-sunny. So each sunny line can contain at most 1 of these points. Thus with 2 sunny lines, at most 2 points can be covered, not 3. Therefore k=2 impossible. This argument is rigorous.

Now, we need to also consider the possibility that we don't take the best possible non-sunny lines; we could take a different set of n-2 lines that might leave a complement of size >3. That would be even worse. So if we can't cover the minimum complement (size 3) with 2 sunny lines, then certainly any configuration with 2 sunny lines cannot cover all points, because the uncovered points after non-sunny lines will be at least 3. But is it possible that with a different selection of non-sunny lines, the complement size is 2 or 1, making it easier? The complement size after n-2 non-sunny lines is at least |S_n| - U_max. Since U_max is the maximum possible union, the complement size is at least 3. So it's impossible to have complement size <3. Therefore with n-2 non-sunny lines, at least 3 points are uncovered. So k=2 impossible.

Now, what about k>3? That would be k sunny lines and n-k non-sunny lines. For n≥4, n-k is at least 1. The complement after non-sunny lines is at least |S_n| - U_max(n-k). That is, the minimum possible uncovered points is |S_n| - U_max(n-k). If we can show that this minimum is > k * M (where M is max points per sunny line), then k>3 impossible. But we need a more precise argument. Actually we want to show that for any k≥4, it's impossible. For n large, n-k might be small. But the complement size may be large. But we can use the fact that with n-k non-sunny lines, the maximum union is (n-k)(2n+1-(n-k))/2 = (n-k)(n+k+1)/2. Then the minimum uncovered is |S_n| - that = n(n+1)/2 - (n-k)(n+k+1)/2 = [n(n+1) - (n-k)(n+k+1)]/2. Compute:

n(n+1) = n^2 + n.
(n-k)(n+k+1) = (n-k)*(n+k+1) = (n-k)*(n+k) + (n-k) = (n^2 - k^2) + (n - k). So that's n^2 - k^2 + n - k.
Difference = (n^2 + n) - (n^2 - k^2 + n - k) = k^2 + k = k(k+1).
Thus min uncovered = k(k+1)/2. That's the size of the set of points that cannot be covered by n-k non-sunny lines if we choose the best ones. However, we are not forced to choose the best non-sunny lines; we could choose a set that covers fewer points, leaving more uncovered. That's worse. So the minimum possible uncovered points is k(k+1)/2. But is this achievable? We need to see if there exists a set of n-k non-sunny lines covering exactly that many points. The maximum union is (n-k)(2n+1-(n-k))/2, so the complement is n(n+1)/2 - max union. That complement is the size of points that cannot be covered by any set of n-k non-sunny lines, i.e., the points that are "far" from those lines. If we choose the n-k rows with largest sizes (b=1..n-k), then the complement is rows b=n-k+1 .. n, which has total size sum_{b=n-k+1}^{n} (n+1-b) = sum_{i=1}^{k} i = k(k+1)/2. So indeed, we can achieve that complement size. So the minimum uncovered is k(k+1)/2.

Now, we have k sunny lines. They can cover at most k * M points, where M is the maximum number of points from S_n that can lie on a sunny line. But we need to see if k(k+1)/2 ≤ k * M. If M ≥ (k+1)/2, then k(k+1)/2 ≤ k * M would hold. For k≥4, (k+1)/2 ≥ 2.5. Is M always at least (k+1)/2? M depends on n. For large n, M is about n/2 (from y=x). So for k up to something like n, M could be larger. However we need to prove that it's impossible for k≥4 for any n≥3. The argument above shows that with n-k non-sunny lines, the complement is at least k(k+1)/2. If we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k * M points. So a necessary condition is k(k+1)/2 ≤ k * M, i.e., (k+1)/2 ≤ M. If M is small, this condition may fail. But we need to prove that for any n≥3 and any k≥4, either (k+1)/2 > M or the configuration cannot be realized even if the inequality holds, but we can use the fact that the complement points (the points with b≥n-k+1) are a specific set of size k(k+1)/2, and we need to cover them with k sunny lines. The maximum number of points that k sunny lines can cover from that set is at most k * (maximum points per sunny line). However we need to bound the maximum points per sunny line within that set. But it's possible that a sunny line could contain many points from that set. For example, if k is small, say k=4, the complement is 10 points. The maximum per sunny line is 3. So 4*3=12 >10, so inequality holds. But we need to check if those 10 points can be covered by 4 sunny lines. The complement for n=5 (k=4) is the set of points with b=3,4,5 (size 10). As we argued, the max per sunny line is 2 (since no line contains 3 points from that set). Actually we need to examine whether any sunny line can contain 3 points from that set. The set is: b=3: a=1..3, b=4: a=1..2, b=5: a=1. That's 10 points. Could there be a sunny line with 3 points? Let's test slope 1: y=x gives (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) – only (2,2) and (3,3) are in set, (1,1) not. So 2. Slope 2: (1,2) not, (2,4) – (2,4) is in set, (3,6) not. So 1. Slope -2: (2,4) and (3,2) gives 2. So max appears to be 2. So with 4 sunny lines, at most 8 points can be covered. Therefore impossible. So the argument based solely on bound M might not suffice because M may be larger than the actual maximum per line within that specific complement. But we can analyze the geometry more generally.

We want to show that for any n≥3, if k≥4, then any configuration of n lines covering S_n must have at least 3 sunny lines? Actually we want to prove that k=2,3 are possible, and k=0,1 are possible, and k>3 impossible. But we already have a construction for k=3, so k>3 is impossible if we can prove that any covering with n lines requires at most 3 sunny lines. But is it possible to have k=4 for n=3? n=3, k=4 would be 4 sunny lines, but only 3 lines total. So impossible. For n=4, k=4 would be 4 sunny lines, that's k=n. We already argued impossible because with 4 sunny lines, max coverage is something like 4*M where M is max per sunny line; but we need a rigorous proof that no set of 4 sunny lines can cover S_4. The bound M for n=4 is 2 (from y=x) or 3 (from y=-2x+7). Actually we found a sunny line with 3 points (y=-2x+7). So M≥3. But 4*3=12 >10, so not ruled out. But we need to prove that it's impossible to cover S_4 with 4 sunny lines. We can attempt to use a combinatorial argument based on the fact that each sunny line can contain at most something like n points? That's not enough. But we already have a construction with 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line that works. That's k=3. So k=4 is impossible for n=4. Similarly for n=5, k=4 impossible. So indeed, for n≥4, k=n is impossible. But we need to prove that for all n≥3, k>3 is impossible. The argument for k=2 used the fact that with n-2 non-sunny lines, the complement is at least 3, and those 3 points cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines. For k=3, we have n-3 non-sunny lines, complement size at least 6. The complement of the best non-sunny lines (rows 1..n-3) is 6 points. That complement can be covered by 3 sunny lines, as shown. So k=3 is possible. For k=4, we have n-4 non-sunny lines. The complement size after the best selection (rows 1..n-4) would be rows n-3,n-2,n-1,n → size 4+3+2+1 = 10. That's 10 points. With 4 sunny lines, is it possible to cover those 10 points? The maximum per sunny line might be something like 3, but we need to see if 4*3=12 >10, so not ruled out. However we can try to prove that in any covering with n lines, the number of sunny lines cannot exceed 3. Perhaps by considering that the sum of the row sizes of the rows that are not covered by non-sunny lines is at least something, and then using a bound on the maximum number of points that sunny lines can cover from that set. The complement after n-k non-sunny lines is a set of points that lie in the "bottom" of the triangle (the rows with large b). For n-k non-sunny lines, the best we can do is to take the rows b=1..n-k, which covers the top part. The remaining rows are b=n-k+1..n. Their total size is k(k+1)/2. So the set of points that must be covered by sunny lines (since they are not covered by non-sunny lines) is at least that size. However, we could also use non-sunny lines that are not all rows, but we can always choose the best non-sunny lines to minimize uncovered points. The configuration we are trying to construct for a given k would have n-k non-sunny lines, but we are free to choose which lines. The most efficient way to cover points with non-sunny lines is to take rows b=1..n-k, which leaves exactly k(k+1)/2 uncovered. That's the minimal possible uncovered. So if we want to have k sunny lines, we need to be able to cover those k(k+1)/2 points with k sunny lines. The question is: for which k is this possible? We know that for k=3, it's possible (with the pairing we gave). For k=4, we need to check if there exists a set of 4 sunny lines that together contain all points of a set of 10 points (the rows b=n-3,n-2,n-1,n). That set is a triangular arrangement of size 10. But is it possible that those 10 points are covered by 4 sunny lines? The maximum per sunny line might be 3, but we need to see if any sunny line can contain 3 points from that set. For n large, the set of points with b≥n-3 includes rows of sizes: b=n-3 has size 3 (a=1..3), b=n-2 has size 2, b=n-1 has size 1, b=n has size 1? Actually careful: For n, rows b = n-3, n-2, n-1, n. The sizes:

- b = n-3: a ≤ n+1 - (n-3) = 4 → size 4? Wait compute: For b = n-3, a_max = n+1 - (n-3) = 4. So row size = 4. That's for n≥3. Example n=7, b=4 (n-3) size 4? Actually n=7, b=4 => a_max = 7+1-4 = 4 → size 4. But earlier we said rows b=4,5,6,7 for n=7, but we considered complement after rows 1..n-3 (i.e., rows 1..4). That left rows 5,6,7. For n=7, rows b=5,6,7 sizes 3,2,1. So that's 6 points. For k=4, we would have n-k = 3 non-sunny lines. The best non-sunny lines are rows 1..3, leaving rows 4..7, which is 4+3+2+1=10 points. So the set of rows is b = 4,5,6,7. The sizes are 4,3,2,1. That's 10 points. So the set is a triangular arrangement of size 10. For n=6, k=4 would give n-k=2 non-sunny lines, best rows 1..2 leaving rows 3..6 sizes 4,3,2,1 = 10. So the set of points to cover with sunny lines is the set of points with b ≥ n-3 (since n-k = 3). That's rows b = n-3, n-2, n-1, n. The sizes are 4,3,2,1? Actually row b = n-3 has size 4? That's consistent.

Now, can we cover these 10 points with 4 sunny lines? We need to see if there exists a selection of 4 sunny lines that together contain all these points. The points are:

For row b = n-3: (1,n-3),(2,n-3),(3,n-3),(4,n-3)

(2,n-2) covered, (3,n-2) not; from row n-1, we have (1,n-1),(2,n-1) maybe (2,n-1) covered? (3,n-1) covered, but (2,n-1) not; from row n, we have (1,n) covered. So remaining points: (2,n-3),(3,n-3),(4,n-3); (1,n-2),(3,n-2); (1,n-1),(2,n-1); and (1,n) already covered. That's 7 points. But we have 3 sunny lines left. Can we cover those 7 with 3 sunny lines?
